 KUNGL. VITTERHETS HISTORIE OCH ANTIKVITETS AKADEMIEN STOCKHOLM REVELATIONES SANCTAE BIRGITTAE REVELACIONES LIB. IV EDIDIT HANS AILI 
 
 
 
 
 SANCTA BIRGITTA REVELACIONES BOOK IV EDITED BY HANS AILI 
 
 
 
 
 Published with the aid of grants from the Swedish Council for Research in the Humanities and Social Sciences AILI, H., Sancta Birgitta, Reuelaciones, Book IV 416 pp., Svenska Fornskriftsällskapet and Kungl. Vitterhets Historie och An- tikvitets Akademien, Stockholm 1992. ISBN 91-971445-2-1. This critical edition of Liber IV of the Reuelaciones of Saint Birgitta of Sweden, like its predecessors in this series, bases its text upon the readings of selected, medieval manuscripts. The text of the Editio princeps (impr. B. Ghotan, Liibeck 1491) has been superseded, particularly in those ten Revelations of Liber IV that were also incorporated by Alfonso of Jaen into Liber VIII (Liber celestis Imperatoris ad reges); in this case, the present edition presents a text that has as yet been inaccessible to non-specialist scholars. This edition also contains an Apparatus criticus, an Apparatus fontium, a Bibliography, an Introduction on the history of the manuscripts and the text, a Glossary, and Indices of grammar, literary sources, names and places. Keywords: St. Birgitta, St. Bridget, Revelation, Vadstena, Spirituality. Hans Aili, Department of Classical Languages, Stockholm University, S-106 91 STOCKHOLM, Sweden ISSN 0347-9145 ISBN 91-971745-2-1 Printed by Graphic Systems, Göteborg, Sweden 1992

 Preface The editor of a Latin work the size and compiexity of the present edition invariably owes a debt of gratitude to many superiors, colleagues, and friends. My thanks are first of all due to the Royal Academy of Letters, History and Antiquities (Kungl. Vitterhets Historie och Antikvitets Akademien) and in particular to the members of its Birgitta Committee, Professors Birger Bergh, Lund, and Jan Öberg, Stock- holm, who entrusted the task of editing this book to me and who have at all times taken a lively and constructive interest in my work, aiding me in its progress. My thanks are also due to all the members of the Latin seminars of Stockholm and Lund to whom I have on various occasions submitted problematical passages for discussion. Professor Jan-Olof Tjäder, Uppsala, has read my manuscript on behaif of the Swedish Humanistic Research Council, kindly giving it its imprimatur. Mr Hugh Rodwell, M.A., M.Litt. (Cantab.), corrected and much improved my Eng- lish in the Introduction. Needless to say, such errors as may remain are entirely my own responsibility. Work on this edition of the Fourth Book of the Reuelaciones Sanctae Birgittae was begun in 1980 by Dr Anders Piltz, Lund, who collated two manuscripts, F and K, and added numerous references to Birgitta's literary sources, before relin- quishing the task to me, at the end of the same year. Since the collating of many manuscripts unavoidably necessitates revision of earlier collations, I am alone responsible for all notes on the manuscript readings reported in the apparatus criticus. I have, however, gratefully availed myself of the wide learning of Dr Piltz by incorporating all his notes of Birgitta's literary sources into my Apparatus fontium; Dr Birgit Klockars's' references have also been taken fully into account: the Apparatus fontium thus to a great part consists of sources noted by these two scholars, as weil as (and to a lesser extent) of sources traced by myself. Financial support for the work of preparing and printing this edition has been generously given by Statens humanistisk-samhällsvetenskapliga forskningsråd (HSFR); the Institute for Classical Languages of Stockholm has provided me with a study, works of reference, and my first word-processor. Grants for the further computerisation of the text were most generously provided by Magn. Bergvalls fond and Sven och Dagmar SalUs stiftelse. Furthermore, Axel W. Ahlbergs fond, KLOCKARS (1966:255ff.) 
 
 
 
 
 8 Magn. Bergvalls fond, Fondazione Famiglia Rausing, Sven och Dagmar SalUs stiftelse, Wallenbergsstiftelsens jubileumsfond, and Stiftelsen Knut och Alice Wal- lenberg have most generously provided me with the economic means to study Birgitta manuscripts in the following libraries outside Sweden: Staatsbibliotek der Stiftung Preussischer Kulturbesitz of Berlin, Hessische Landes- und Hochschulbi- bliothek of Darmstadt, the Pierpont Morgan Library of New York, Biblioteca centrale della regione Siciliana of Palermo, the Vatican Library, Biblioteca Mar- ciana of Venice, and Österreichische Nationalbibliothek of Vienna. I would like to extend my heartfelt thanks both to the foundations enumerated and to the staff of all these libraries, where I have received much competent help in my studies. I owe a special obligation of gratitude to Dr Per Ekström of Lund University Library for all the help he has given me in conjunction with my manuscript studies at Lund. At the Royal Library of Stockholm and the Uppsala University Library, I have received much help from the staff of the Manuscript Departments. The Stockholm University Library has been my primary source of books. The Swedish Institute (Svenska institutet), by awarding me a travelling grant, made it possible for me to study the Birgitta manuscripts in Prague: my thanks are therefore due not only to the Institute but also to the staff of the Nårodni Muzeum, the Statni Knihovna and the Knihovna Metropolitny Kapitoly. Restat, ut Christinae uxori meae Danielque et Erico filiis, qui patrem eundem- que maritum laborantem, animo occupatum, de operis fortuna desperantem sem- per confirmaverint, iuverint, foverint, hunc librum maximis cum gratiis dedicem. Scriptum in Wadzstenom die sanctae Birgittae anno Domini MCMXCI, qui annus sescentesimus fuit a Birgitta canonizata. Hans Aili

 1 Introduction 1.1 The History of the Text The text of the Reuelaciones of Book IV is divided into three separate parts, each with its own history of transmission. Both the exact composition of the first identifiable arrangement of the text, the so-called Liber Alfonsi, and the dating of this version and the later additions have been the subject of much debate among scholars, notably SCHUCK (1901:22f.), WESTMAN (1911:23ff,), WussES (1952:XVII, XXXVIIf.), HOLLMAN (1956:28), EKWALL (1965:82ff.), and BERGH (1967:17f.; 1975:13f.). UNDHAGEN, in his introduction to Reuelaciones Liber I (1978:14-37), summarises and assesses all the preceding discussion, while JÖNSSON (1989:85ff.) adds new arguments with respect to dating the Revelations. In the following pre5cis, I mainly follow UNDHAGEN, with certain specific reservations. Briefly, it is known for certain that a number of copies of the first edition of the Revelations were in existence in 1377, when they were presented to Pope Gregory IX and to the members of the canonisation commission he had appointed. The text had by then been revised and edited by Birgitta's Spanish confessor, Alfonso Pecha de Vadaterra, ex-bishop of Ja&11 . After the death of Gregory IX in March, 1378, a new commission was appointed by his successor, Urban VI. The Liber Alfonsi in its first redaction apparently contained the Prologus Magist- ri Mathiae and Rev I—VII (with 130 chapters in Book IV and 109 chapters in Book VI). About the year 1380, accorcling to UNDHAGEN'S theory (1978:16fL)', Alfonso revised this book, producing the second Alfonso Redaction, which contained Prol. M., Rev, I--VII, Epistola Solitarii ad reges, Liber Celestis Imperatoris ad reges (= Rev. VIII), Sermo angelicus, and Quatuor oraciones. This is the text given by the Continental manuscripts, PVY BOAN. In the Liber Alfonsi, Book IV consisted of Chapters 1-130. This is clear from the Explicit following Rev. IV 130 in the manuscripts ured for this edition as weil as in Gh, which, with slight variations, state either Explicit liber quartus celestis reuelationurn, or Et hic est finis quarti libri secunduin Alfonsum. His life and works have been amply discussed by JONSSON ( 1989). balel this view on the facts that Book VII in its Explicit is generally called ultimas Liber celestis, and that Book VII is generally called fiber ultimas also in the A &P. JoNssoN's objeetion (1989:86 with note 19), to the effect that Alfons() in une passage in A&P (p. 375, 1. 32) himself calls the Liber celestis Imperatoris ad reges "Liber VIII", is pointless, since the reading in question, vie, is a later correction, the original reading of the manuscript being er. UNDIIAGHN 
 
 
 
 
 18 Besides the material accepted by Alfonso into the textual redaction intended for the canonisation proceedings, the Birgittine corpus contained a large number of revelations, whose contents Alfonso had judged to be too controversial or too mundane to merit inclusion. This material was, however, accepted into the text corpus by later editors. Among these later inclusions are the so called Addiciones and Declaraciones, amplifying the text and commenting on it.3 The dates of the Add. and Decl. vary; the editing was done by Prior Petrus', who had returned to Sweden about 1380, seven years after the death of Birgitta. This material is therefore not to be found in the greater part of those manuscripts which have been written on the Continent. The contents of Book IV were later further amplified by yet other Revelations - one on the excellence of the angels (Rev. IV 131), four on the morals of the clergy (Tractatus ad sacerdotes, Rev. IV 132-135), and nine dealing with the importance of the return of the Papal seat to Rome (Tractatus ad summos pontifices, Rev. IV 136-144). This supplementary material was incorporated gradually and with in- dividual variations, which has given rise to two identifiable later redactions, firstly the Vadstena Redactiod , which is characterised by the presence of all the Add. and Decl. and, in Book IV, by Rev. IV 131-144, secondly the Prague Redaction, which is remarkable because of its peculiar arrangement of the order of the various books, chiefly of the Epistola Solitarii and Liber celestis Imperatoris ad reges (= Book VIII), which in this redaction are placed between Book II and Book III. Moreover, Book IV contains ten Revelations whose text was also included, with revisions, in Book VIII, the Liber celestis imperatoris ad reges. These Revelations, which will henceforth be referred to as the "geminated" or "double" Revelations are IV 1, 3, 4, 17, 45, 48, 74, 103, 104, and 105, corresponding to VIII 25, 41, 13, 15, 50, 7, 34, 26, 27, and 28 respectively. In Books II to VII there are in all twenty-four geminated revelations6 . This fact elicited various reactions among the medieval scribes and the editors of the Editio princeps; this is discussed below in Chapter 2.5. The Revelations of Book IV to a great extent belong to Birgitta's life in Rome, after she had left Sweden in 13497 . The material of this book is heterogeneous and shows no discernible traces of editorial arrangement: in the dated list of contents of Book IV, given by LUNDEN (1957 Vol. 11:11-22), Rev. IV 1-2, 4, 23, 76-77, 93, 103-105, 113-115, 121-123, 125-126 are dated before 1349; IV 3, 5-22, 24-75, 78-81, 89, 102, 107, 124, 127-131, 136-144 are dated after 1350 and belong to the time of Birgitta's stay in Rome, while 82-88, 90-92, 94-101, 106, 108-112, These Revelations are IV 16, 17, 23, 32, 34, 51, 52, 55, 68, 71, 81, 93, 102, 107, 113, 114, 121, 122, 125, 127, and 129. 4 Prior Petrus Olavi of Alvastra Cloister in Sweden, cf. UNDIIAGEN (1978:4). 5 For the contents of the Vadstena and Prague Redactions, ef. UNDIIAOFN (1978:30f.) No geminated revelations are to he found in Book 1. WI:SIMAN (1911:23), KRAFT (1929:67). 
 
 
 
 
 19 116-120 have been considered impossible to date. ÖBERG (1969) has shown that IV 33, 78, 79-80, 139, 140, 142, and 143 have originally been drafted as letters to various persons, and still contain epistolary elements. The dating of the oldest version of the geminated Revelations is also a delicate matter, as I have shown elsewhere8, since the additions given by Alfonso considerably modify the wording of these texts. 1.2 The Manuscripts and the Editio princeps: B, F, K, 0, P, v, Y, U21 , Ab, q, u, x, and Gh Berlin, Staatsbibliothek der Stiftung Preussischer Kulturbesitz, MS. theol. Lat. fol. 33, cent. XV, fol. 97R-153R. B = Literature: BERGH (1967:41, 44, 48ff., 89ff.); BERGH (1971:35, 40ff., 50ff., 67f., 75ff.); EKWALL (1965:121); GÖDEL (1916:28); KRAFT (1929:65, 70, 77); NORDENFALK (1961:384 f.); ROSE (1903:891f. No. 820); SCHUCK (1901:22 with note 58)9, UND- HAGEN (1978:14ff., 21f., 170ff., 199); WESTMAN (1911:22 with note 2). B is a parchment manuscript, size ca. 14 by 21 cm, containing 298 folio leaves. The manuscript has probably been cut somewhat in the binding, since traces of catchwords may be observed at the very bottom of some pages. The dating of this manuscript has aroused considerable controversy, the dates assumed for its execution ranging from 1379 to ca. 1460. The former dating is the traditional one, supported by a long notem written on a flyleaf pasted onto the inside cover and giving the early history of the manuscript. Since this note mentions the existence of printed editions of the Revelations, it must be assumed to have been written after the appearance of the Editio princeps in 1492". Briefly, it would appear that the manuscript was written in Rome ca. 1379, on the request of a certain "Frater Petrus de Burgundia, ordinis minorum maximus theologus"12, from Burgundy, and not long afterwards transmitted to Maria, Duchess of Cleves u, who gave it to the newly founded Birgittine convent of Marienboem, about the year 1460. The manuscript bears traces of damage by fire: according to a note on fol. 155R, this fire occurred in 1513. This early dating of B is accepted by ROSE and ScHocK, but rejected with cogent arguments by WESTMAN, NORDENFALK (1961:384) All,' (1986:86 f.). SCHUCK erroneously designates this manuscript Mscr. Germ. fol. 726. 18 The note is printed by ROSE (in a footnote on page 891) and by SCHUCK (note 58); neither scholar mentions the fact that this note, the original wording of which is clearly given by them according to the text on the flyleaf, had already been copied, with many omissions and errors, on the front endpaper of B; an indication of the importance with which this manuscript was regarded by its owners. " For this history, cf. also ROSE (1908:891), NORDENFALK (1961:384) and UNDHAGEN (1978:15 and 171). 12 Petrus' identity is otherwise not known; NORDENFALK'S attempt to identify him with Petrus ad Boves (Pierre aux Boeufs) is rejected by UNDHAGEN (1978:171 note 2), on the ground that the latter Petrus was only about ten years old in 1380. '3 Fourth wife of Henry VIII of England. 
 
 
 
 
 20 and UNDHAGEN (1978:172), who regard it instead as a copy made by Petrus' manuscript. NORDENFALK assumes that the manuscript was copied at Marienboem, after the founding of this convent in 1460; UNDHAGEN, for his part, dates B to the period 1445 to 1467; if a date earlier than 1460 can be accepted for B, then it, together with another Berlin manuscript, also previously in the possession of the Marienboem convent, b, may derive from Marienwater, the mother convent of Marienboem. Since UNDHAGEN (1978:172 with note 12) appears to countenance the theory that B may have been copied from b, although he concedes that he has not compared the respective texts thoroughly, a certain amount of space may be devoted to a discussion of this problem here. UNDHAGEN'S concern is chiefly to explain why B should contain a detailed alphabetical table of contents, otherwise known only from b (=Berlin, Staatsbi- bliothek der Stiftung preul3ischer Kulturbesitz, MS. theol. Lat. fol. 262 and 263), d (=Diisseldorf, Landes- und Stadtbibliothek, MS C94), Gb (=Paderborn, Erzbi- schöfliche Akademische Bibliothek, MS. Pa. 74), and Q (=Wien, Österreichische Nationalbibliothek, MS. 3920), and assumes that this index may have been copied into B from b. To me this appears rather unlikely on the following grounds. The contents of B are typical of a Birgitta manuscript containing the early Alfonsine redaction, SA, Q0. In addition, it contains the alphabe- namely Prol. Matthiae, Rev. tical index mentioned above. The manuscript, like the other manuscripts of this redaction, includes no Addiciones and Declaraciones, nor Rev. IV 131-144; on the other hand, it consistently retains the 24 geminated revelations suo loco in Rev. II, III, IV, V, VI and VII. The same revelations are also written out fully in the Liber ad Reges. In b, on the other hand, Addiciones and Declaraciones are given at the end of Rev. I: 3, 13, 16, 17, 23, 28 and 32; Rev. II: 2; Rev. III: 10, 11, 12, 13, 20, 22, 33; Rev. IV: 16, 17, 23, 32, 34, 51, 52, 55, 68, 71, 81, 93, 102, 107, 113, 114, 121, 122, 125, 127 and 129; Rev. VI: 2, 5, 9, 10, 15, 16, 17, 18, 19, 20, 22, 30, 31, 32, 35, 36, 39, 52, 66, 75, 98, and 102; Rev. VII: 4, 5, 11, and 16. Moreover, of those Revelations that are consistently geminated in B, the following occur only once in b: Rev. II: 6, 9 and 11 (with reference to Liber ad Reges/Book VIII 42, 33 and 37); and Rev. VI: 26 (with reference to Liber ad Reges/Book VIII 30). In Liber ad Reges, the text is written out only in the four instances just mentioned; in VIII 7, 8, 13, 15, 22, 23, 24, 25, 26, 27, 28, 29, 32, 34, 35, 36, 38, 41, 44, 50 and 58 reference is instead made to the corresponding revelations in the earlier books. This is a system which I have not encountered in any other Birgitta manuscript, although it comes very close to the principle followed in the Ghotan edition, where reference is always made from Rev. VIII to the earlier books. Cf. below, ch 2.5. Now, from the presence of the Addiciones, Declaraciones and references in b, it is quite clear that B cannot in its entirety be a copy of b, and it is hardly conceivable that these discrepancies between the two manuscripts should have gone completely unnoticed if a portion of b was indeed copied into B; it is therefore more probable that the indexes in B and b derive from a common source. 
 
 
 
 
 21 On the other hand, the following observation will show that the two manuscripts may have existed side by side in the convent of Marienboem, without being very closely compared as to contents. In the note entered on the flyleaf of B, and mentioned above as a means of dating the manuscript itself, it is observed: Notandum tamen est quod in presenti libro multe alie revelationes diue birgitte facte deficiunt que cum illis inseruntur libris qui imprimuntur In quarto etenim libro deficiunt XIIII revelationes In sexto XIII. Although this note does not mention the Addiciones and Declaraciones, it nonethe- less shows that B and the printed editions had been compared, to a certain extent. The fact that a sister manuscript, now denoted b, contained a great deal of other material that does occur in the printed editions, obviously escaped the writer's notice. "Codex Falkenberg"" appears to be the oldest manuscript used for this edition, although, in my opinion, the arrangement of the text represents a somewhat later stage than that offered by PVY BOA''; it was written in its entirety at Vadstena Abbey, by Brother Johannes Johannis Kalmarnensis (b. ca 1362, d. 1446) t5. A facsimile edition of this manuscript was published by WESSEN (1952, 1956) with an introduction in English (1952:XXXI—LI), where he describes the manuscript and traces its history. He dates F to after the year 1404, on the ground that this was the year when Johannes Johannis, having been ordained a deacon, could first call himself frater. This argument has not been accepted, however. Many years before, DIDERICHSEN (1935:235, 245), discussing among many other things the relative datings of F and K, had concluded that the arrangement of the supplementary material of F argued for a dating earlier than 1390, and that this manuscript thus represented the oldest known arrangement among the Vadstena manuscripts. UNDHAGEN (1978:94) corroborates DIDERICHSEN'S conclusion with the further argu- ment that the arrangement found in K is identical with that of the oldest dated Revelationes manuscript, Fb, written in 1386'. Hence, the arrangement of F '4 So named as it was donated to Lund University Library by Count Melker Falkenberg, in the year 1778, cf. UNDHAGEN (1978:92). Cf.  22 appears to be of an even earlier date. Both DIDERICHSEN and UNDHAGEN consider it unlikely that a scribe of Johannes Johannis' standing would have copied the text in such a disorderly fashion, if he had known of a more consistent arrangement. In this I concur, and I would in addition refer to the observation made below (ch. 2.5), to the effect that F represents the second oldest version of the extant textual arrangements (after PVY BOA") in its treatment of the geminated Revelations. Hence, there are cogent reason for agreeing with UNDHAGEN in regarding F as the oldest existing Vadstena manuscript of the Revelations." Owing to the loss of one entire quire, the text of F is defective in Book IV, there being a lacuna from IV 59:5 (after fugiendo cupiditatem mundi) to 81:3, where the text is resumed with the words quam spiritualem paupertatem. Kalmar, Stifts- och gymnasiebiblioteket, at present in Lund University Libra- ry, "Codex Kalmarnensis" (or "Calmariensis"), cent. XIV late, fol. 64R-112R. K = Literature: BERGH (1967:61 ff.); COLLIJN (1923:12); BRING (1931:225); DIDERICHSEN (1935 :234ff.); EDMAR (1933); HOLLMAN (1956:47f.); KRAFT (1926:160), (1929:63ff.); LINNARSSON (1931:25f.); WALDE (1932:2 f.); WESTMAN (1913:23ff.) In Book IV, as in the other books, the text of K has been corrected throughout by a second hand, K2 , not identical with K; in addition, K2 at the end of the codex (fol. 250V-257V) adds the complete text of the geminated Revelations'8, except for IV 104 (the text after §6 habet os ante) and the whole of IV 105, since a leaf appears to have fallen out of the manuscript after fol. 256V — a loss which does not show in the modern pagination —; on fol. 257R, the text is resumed with IV 121. Besides the text of the geminated revelations, K2 gives the text of IV 17 Add. 19 , 121 Decl., 127 Decl., and 129 Add. WESSEN'S argument that Johannes Johannis could not have been called frater until 1404 is effectively refuted by BERGH (1967:61), who points out that Johannes is called frater in a document dated 1402, and that WESSEN himself quotes this document.  23 O = Oxford, Balliol College Library, MS. 225, after 1424, fol. 64R-111R. Literature: BERGH (1967:42, 45ff., 89ff.), (1971:34ff., 41, 46, 51f., 65ff.); COXE (1852:71); FRERE (1901:150); MYNORS (1963:224f.); ScHocK (1887:165); UNDHAGEN (1960:36-43, 118-128; 133 ff.), (1978:185f., 196, 198f., 225). This manuscript was first described in detail by UNDHAGEN (1960 and, in English, 1978) as a folio size parchment manuscript, which originally contained 224 leaves (fol. 1 is now lost), written by one hand throughout. UNDHAGEN links the genesis of this manuscript with the English theologian, Thomas Gascoigne, Chancellor of Oxford University; it was probably copied at his request at Syon Abbey from an exemplar which reached this monastery in 1424; the marginal notes to be found in 0 can be traced to his hand. P = Palermo, Biblioteca centrale della regione siciliana (formerly Biblioteca nazio- nale), Ms. IV.G.2, cent. XIV late, fol. 58R —97V. Literature: BERGH (1967:55f., 58ff., 68f), (1971:54ff.); COLLIJN (1924,IV note 1); EKLUND (1972:28 f., 1975:35ff.); LATTANZI (1955, 1965:62 ff.); LUND (1957 1:44); NORDENFALK (1961:373 ff.); SCHUCK (1901:54 note 59); UNDHAGEN (1978:150ff.); WESTMAN (1911:270). Modern scholars agree in dating this parchment manuscript of 37.5 by 26 cm, containing 209 leaves, to the years immediately preceding Birgitta's canonisation in 1391 and trace its origins to Naples. In modern times, this manuscript was first mentioned in 1771 by Salvatore Maria di Blasi, librarian to the monastery of San Martino delle Scale near Palermo, who reviewed an ancient catalogue of the manuscripts contained in this library.2`) The identity of P with the manuscript mentioned in this catalogue was confirmed by LATTANZI (1955), although the catalogue was missing when she wrote her paper; she added the important point that the catalogue note on this manuscript cannot have been entered before 1386. The manuscript catalogue having since been found, the identity of the two manuscripts is reaffirmed by BERGH (1967:55). NORDENFALK'S suggestion (1961:379) that P and Y (which are remarkably similar in the execution of their illuminations) might have been completed as many as five or ten years earlier than has commonly been supposed is difficult to assess: it is founded on an observation made in the typewritten description of Y in the Morgan Library, to the effect that the style of the costumes and helmets depicted date from about 1375; but this is a peculiarity which may only be regarded as a terminus ante quem non. LATTANZI (1955:10) considers the angels depicted in P to be "rigidi, legnosi, quasi privi di muscoli", a style indicative of a late period of the Naples school of illumination, and she dates P to the final decade of the 14th century. Di Blasi, S.M., "Relazione della nuova libreria del Monastero di S. Martino delle Scale", Opuscoli di autori siciliani, 1771, T. XII, 186-190. 1)

BERGH (1967:37f.), argues that the textual evidence in r and V indicates that the latter may be a direct copy of the former. The dates given for the manuscripts naturally preclude the opposite hypothesis. BERGII'S view is contested by EKLUND (1972:26f.), mainly on the strength of evidence in the text of SA, where r exhibits some ten errors not shared by V, but he also disagrees with BERGH'S evaluation of the textual evidence in Rev. VII. UNDHAGE N (1978:156), who appears not to have noticed EKLUND'S discussion on the relationship between r and V, concurs in BERGH's conclusions on this subject, and traces the following tentative early history 
 
 
 
 
 25 of V: r was given by Abbot Ypolitus of the monastery of San Girolamo di Quarto outside Genoa to a fellow-monk, Johannes de Alamania, who copied Rev. I, QO, Rev. II—IV for his own use, thereby creating V; the second part of r: SA and Rev. V—VIII (cf. UNDHAGEN 1960:49) was copied by another monk, Lodouicus, who wrote the Explicit to Rev. VIII, dated 1399. This tentative history is contradicted by the individual errors exhibited by r against V in the text of SA, as observed by EKLUND (1972;26 f.); the latter scholar in fact regards these two manuscripts as descended from a common exemplar. Very clear evidence, supporting BERGH'S view on the dependence of V on r, has come to light during my own collation of these two manuscripts for my projected edition of Book VIII. In r, the whole of VIII 53 has been left out by the scribe. The omission apparently has occurred in the following manner: VIII 52 ends on fol. 202V, filling out the whole page, which is the final one of its quire. The scribe has then added the first words of the Rubric of VIII 53 (Christus per sponsam) at the bottom of the page as a catchword for the next quire (fol. 203R). Unfortunately, the Rubric of VIII 54 also begins with exactly the same words. When starting to write on the first page of the new quire, the scribe apparently therefore looked up the chapter whose Incipit corresponded to the catchword, but failed to notice that he had selected VIII 54 and not, as he should have done, VIII 53. In V, VIII 53 is also completely lacking; the text of VIII 52 ends in the middle of a column and is followed by the Rubric and text of VIII 54. Noticing, as it appears, the error in the numbering of the chapters, the scribe corrected the inconsistency by re-numbering all chapters to the end of this book, which, as a consequence, has erroneous chapter numbers from ch. 53 (= VIII 54) to ch. 57 (= VIII 58). The absence of VIII 53 and the consequent re-numbering of the chapters in V can only be understood in the light of the particular circumstances surrounding the error committed by the scribe of r; pence, it is safe to conclude that V was copied directly or indirectly — from r. This observation is most important for the present edition of Reuelaciones, Book IV, as it confirms that V may be used in the place of r as a textual witness for those Revelations that are lacking in the latter, namely Rev. I—IV. In this part of the corpus, V thus appears to be the sole surviving representative of a problematical branch of the tradition. As will be clear from the apparatus criticus of the present edition, u, despite its late date of writing, gives a text which is markedly superior to that much older Prague manuscript, x; it is therefore an excellent example of the philologist's dictum: "recentiores, non deteriores". In fact, at the time when I discovered u, thanks to an almost fortuitous visit to the National Museum of Prague, I had already collated two other manuscripts of the Prague redaction, v and 1b32, both considerably older than u. When the latter manuscript was collated by me, it turned out that x, v, and Ib not only contained nearly all the errors of u but also shared a great number of additional errors not to be found in u. The discovery of this manuscript therefore lead to a radical revision of the apparatus, where the readings of v and Ib, which I had not had the opportunity of verifying suo loco, were completely discarded and only those of x, which had been the original reason for my visiting Prague, were retained. 32 v = Warszawa, Biblioteka Narodowa, MS. 17067, a. 1400; cf. The history of the printing of the Editio princeps has been traced by UNDHAGEN33 and need only be summarised here. The editors, Brother Petrus Ingemarsson and Lay Brother Gerard, belonged to the mother foundation of the Birgittine Order, Vadstena Abbey. They set out from Vadstena on the hundredth anniversary of St. Birgitta's canonisation, October 7th, 1491, to arrange for the first printing of the Revelations, by Bartholomxus Ghotan of Liibeck. According to the postscript of Gh, the abbey fathers were responsible for the collection and arrangement of the Revelations into this volume, which was intended to be authoritative. The printed work appeared in the autumn of 1492. UNIMIAGEN (1978:1-4 and passim) with further rcfcrenccs. 
 
 
 
 
 2 The Manuseripts and their Mutual Relations 2,0 Main Corpus: PVY, BOAb, Kux, and Fq Gh The manuscripts and the Editio princeps in which the text has been handed down to us can be arranged into four different groups, held together by distinctive textual errors. In those editions of the Reuelaciones which have already been published, the editors, HOLLMAN, BERGH, EKLUND, and UNDHAGEN, have found conjunctive errors that link these four manuscript groups into two main divisions, PVY BOAb (13) and Kux Fq Gh (n)34 , desccnding from their archetype, the text of which can be reconstructed on the strength of the readings common to both branches of the manuscript tradition. In Book IV, also, the existence of the archetype is confirmed by the fact that all the manuscripts share obvious textual errors, which can only have been handed down through a common exemplar. These errors are discussed below in the sections on textual criticism, chs. 3.2 and X3.3. Furthermore, a small number of apparent errors, shared by the 13 manuscripts, and another set of errors, shared by the manuscripts, would confirm that the textual transmission of Book IV, like that of the other Revelations books, is fundamentally bifurcated. There are seventy-odd places where the reading of PVY BOAb differs from that of Kux Fq Gh, but nearly all of them must be regarded as variant readings rather than as textual errors. The sheer number of variants, shared by PVY and BOAb on the one hand and by Kux, Fq, and Gh on the other, in itself argues that there must at one time have existed two separate hyparchetypes of the text. However, only in a few instances is it possible to find errors with a certain conjunctive force. PVY BOAb as a group are only held together by the following errors. IV 22:4 deus diues factus est egenus etc.] deus factus est diues egenus etc. PVY BOAb: diues obviously serves as a predicate attributs; ("God, although rich, has been made destitutc"), hence the reading of p is nonsensical. IV 80:6 solucionem n] solucione VY BOAb lucione P. In the full sentence, in is obviously meant to govern the accusative; hence the readings of VY BOAb and P u was not described in print or collated until the present edition. 
 
 
 
 
 33 appear erroneous, although the error — the loss of a linea nasalis — could easily occur spontaneously. IV 85:3 defendet n] defendit Errors of verbal tense or mood cannot always be regarded as disrupting the sense of a medieval Latin text. In the Revelations there is furthermore ample evidence that the classical rules for consecutio temporum were not always strictly observee. Nevertheless, the context of the above instance is such as to suggest that the reading of [3 is a real error on the part of the Latin translator; hence, we may attribute a certain binding force to the error of tense here. Altogether, then, we may regard these six manuscripts, PVY BOAb, as descen- dants of a common hyparchetype, namely Kux Fq Gh are also bound together by shared errors: facta confessa Kux Fq. Facta is not only IV 7:10 confessa PVY BOAb superfluous but introduces an anacoluthon; it has been removed from the text of Gh, although it must have been present in its exemplars. It is possible that this was originally an archetype error, corrected in the 13 hyparchetype. However that may be, its presence in Kux Fq can only be explained by the assumption that it was present in their common ancestor, n. IV 37:3 relinquentur PV BOAb relinquetur Y] relinquuntur n. This verb is the predicate of one of a series of three coordinated main clauses; since the two remaining clauses are in the future I, the tense exhibited by n is an error on a par with that adduced above for the p hyparchetype. IV 68:6 paciencia] paciencie n: the correct reading must be paciencia virtus "patience appears to be a virtue but is not, ostenditur et non est, quando scilicet when ...", which is the reading of PVY and BOA'', the reading paciencie of Kux and q Gh (this chapter is missing in F) gives "the virtue of patience is demonstrated but does not exist ..." which is meaningless in this context; on the other hand, the error was also easily corrected at all stages of the transmission and therefore has no very strong conjunctive force. The readings offered by Kux Fq Gh in these three passages, taken together, are a fairly strong sign of the existence of a hyparchetype. Hence, we may on the whole conclude that the textual tradition of Book IV of the Reuelaciones is fundamentally bifurcated, one branch, dubbed n, being represented by Kux Fq Gh, while the other, named 13, is represented by PVY BOA': the latter branch suffers from a certain uncertainty about the exact paths of transmission of the text from the hyparchetype. 35 Cf. below, Index locutionum, s.v. Tenses. 
 
 
 
 
 34 2.1 The p group of manuscripts The uncertainty concerning the 13 hyparchetype is shown in several ways, discussed below. 2.1.1 The f group: PVY This is in many respect the most important group of manuscripts, both for under- standing the tradition of the text and for the history of art in relation to St. Birgitta. With respect to the text, V and Y separately are closely connected to the monastery of San Girolamo di Quarto sulla Riperia, where the chief redactor of the Revela- tions text, the ex-bishop Alfonso of Ja&i, spent the final years of his life.3 With respect to art history, P and Y, both of Neapolitan origins, contain illuminated initials, introducing each Revelations book, that have influenced later conceptions of the Swedish saint. This is even more true of the two full-page illuminations of Y, which alone survive to testify to the beginnings of an entire tradition of church art.37 The textual history of these three manuscripts is somewhat complicated. There are some 40 variant readings unique to this group, but the number of really conjunctive errors is rather small. IV 7:63 dei presenciam et eius visionem beatam] deum PVY. IV 9:9 cinctus] cintus PVY: this spelling probably reflects the Italian origins of the manuscripts; cf. It. "cinto"! IV 17:21 quid vero est caro nisi cibus vermium om. PVY: an error by omission of a kind that would not invite correction, as it leaves out only one of the rhetorical questions in this passage; nor is it likely that this phrase was spontaneously restored to the other manuscripts. IV 58:13 occupentur] occupent PVY IV 65:5 meum om. PVY IV 74:53 apostolus om. PVY IV 102:11 augustinus PV ante torr. Y On the strength of these errors we may conclude that the manuscripts PVY have descended from a common exemplar, called p, according to the convention of the modern editions of the Reuelaciones. Unfortunately, the textual tradition of this group, as has been mentioned above, is not quite so straightforward as this statement would imply, since there is ample evidence of separate ties between PY against V, of VY against P and of VY2 against PY. Firstly, there is evidence of a close affinity between P and Y. Examples of this affinity abound in the text of Book IV. Here follows a short list of selected instances. The latter branch will be discussed below. In the branch, V and Y present several complications, which indicate that these two manuscripts have been the subject of collateral influences from manuscripts not belonging to this branch. Although V and Y both clearly belong to the 3 group of manuscripts, each of them appears separately to have undergone a process of correction. Y seems to have received collateral readings in two stages: in its first copying, when errors and variants also to be found in V (but not in P) were introduced into the text itself, secondly when Y2 painstakingly corrected the text according to a source that appears closely related to V. In one instance, however, the reading presented by Y appears to give the clue to the reading of Alfonso's original. This passage is discussed below, ch. 3.2. V presents a different picture. As BERGH has demonstrated42, this may have been a copy of the Vatican ms. r43 . EKLUND44 , examining the text of Sermo angelicus, instead finds evidence that both r and V may have descended from a common source; a conclusion contradicted by my own findings in Book VIII (see above p. 25). In Book IV, this problem is actually insoluble, since r appears to have been bound in two volumes and the first of these (containing Book IV) has been lost. Hence, whenever the critical editions establish a Latin text older than that of Gh, this fact should be faithfully reported for the benefit of scholarship. Cf. also below, ch. 4. The position of Gh within the stemma codicum is less clear cut than could be desired; in Book IV it cannot, as UNDHAGEN avers for his edition of Book I", be regarded as codicis instar. Instead, it is a text compiled from manuscripts close to K and F, but not lineally descended from the n hyparchetype, as the following examples will indicate. IV 76:14 pars et] pauperes K pauperes et Gh: a most significant error on the part of K Gh. The double meaning of the Greek word xkfeo; ("lot, piece of land, inheritance", and "clergy"), borrowed into Church Latin in the form clerus, must have been perfectly clear to both Birgitta herself and her translators'. The Old Swedish version reflects this awareness55 (BU 11:149, 15 "Än huat äru klärkane ytan gudz lutir oc almosomän" — "What else are the clergy but God's heritage and almsmen"). The archetype of the passage under discussion undoubtedly read pars, a word which is at variance with three parallel passages, where sors' is the word used; as it is a matter of doubt whether the reading pars is erroneous for sors or a less successful translation of clerus, I have retained it in the text. The similarities shared by K Gh, on the other hand, are ollen due to a use of synonyms or variant 
 
 
 
 
 41 forms. Since, finally, both K and F contain errors of omission not shared by either the other manuscripts or Gh, we must conclude that the Vadstena brothers who edited Gh must have used as their source at least two manuscripts, closely related to but not identical with K and F, and that they selected their text from these two sources, and possibly others, too. In the case of the text rubrics, which are consistently lacking in F in Rev. IV 1-130, the exemplar of Gh cannot but be K or a manuscript closely related to it. 2.3 Addiciones et Declaraciones: Kqux qUa F Gh The Addiciones and Declaraciones have not reached us in a consistent fashion. In the manuscripts used for the main part of this edition, these sections of the text are completely absent from the 13 manuscripts; they are most consistently handed down by K55 (or K2)59, q, F60, and Gh, to a slightly lesser degree by Ua61, and more sporadically by ux62. The question of the relationship between these manuscripts is complicated by the shortness of the texts involved and by the lack of significant errors. There is, moreover, no reason to expect all Addiciones and Declaraciones to have been added to the text at the same time and by the same path; hence relationships observed within one section of the text may not be valid in others. One constant trait may nevertheless be noted, namely the consistency with which q and Ua share errors, such as: IV 32:8 trinus et F deus et q et deus Ua om. K Gh. Despite the varied word order, the readings of q and Ua are obviously wrong in their context, while it is very difficult to decide whether it is F or K Gh that give the authentic reading. IV 81:35 quadam om. qUa IV 81:46 tria post consideranda tpt K om. qUa In Rev. IV 127, ux and q share a significant error: 7 saccum] scutum qux. IV 127:4-27, whereas there is no way of ascertaining the position of Ua in this section of the text. IV 55:8 earum] eorum K F: the reading of K and F is impossible in its context, since the antecedent of the pronoun is mulieres. The error could easily occur through a simple slip of the pen, however. IV 1, 3, 4, 17, 45, 48, 74, and 103-105 Originally, the Liber Alfonsi contained only Books I to VII, as is evident from the Explicit found in the manuscripts of the 11 group". To these seven books Alfonso later added an eighth, the Liber celestis imperatoris ad reges, which he made up partly from Revelations contained in the earlier books, partly from such Revela- tions as he had excluded from the first collectionTM. This had the effect that twenty-four Revelations' occurred twice over, first in Books II to VII', then in Book VIII; this fact must soon have been noted by the scribes, who probably considered it unnecessary to write the same text twice. This in turn gave rise to the question of which text to exclude: that in Books II to VII or that in Book VIII. Among modern scholars KRAFT (1929:77ff.) has discussed this phenomenon in detail; assuming that the Liber celestis originally contained only Book I—VII and that the Liber celestis ad reges was a separate collection altogether67, he concluded that the exclusion of one of each of the geminated Revelations began after their having been merged into one codex, that is, the one which is now known as the "Second Alfonso Redaction"68. KRAFT based his discussion on a study of the arrangement of the Revelations in B, F, K, and Gh, but did not notice the textual variations of the Revelations involved: the latter point is important, as it is pos- sible, through a comparison of these versions, to trace the development and character of Alfonso's revisions'. I shall return to this question below. KRAFT'S Cf. SCHUCK (1901:22ff.), whose arguments have been reinforced by UNDHAGEN (1978:16ff.) Cf. WESTMAN (1911:25f.), KRAFT (1929:77ff.), BERGH (1967:18), UNDHAGEN (1978:29 f.), Atu (1986), JÖNSSON (1989:58ff.). 65 These are the Revelations which have been moved more or less in full into Book VIII; in addition to these, Alfonso compiled Rev. VIII 1 from passages in Rev. VII 30, III 26, II 13, II 18, and II 7, cf. KRAFT (1929:134ff.). Furthermore, in the printing, Book VIII was amplified by Rev. VIII 59-61, the text of which was copied from the Extravagantes. 66 No such texts are to be found in Rev. I. 67 This theory was formulated by SCHOCK (1901:19f.) and accepted by WESTMAN (1911:25f). 68 UNDHAGEN (1978:22ff.). 69 Cf. AILI (1986). 63 64 
 
 
 
 
 44 discussion is too brief and, lacking a tabular arrangement of the facts, rather difficult to follow'. The solution adopted by the various scribes appears from Table 1 (below, p. 55). If the consistency, or lack of it, with which the Revelations of Book II—VII have been retained suo loco or referred away to Book VIII may be used as an indicator allowing us to trace the history of the redactions, Table 1 would permit the following conclusions. Firstly, all the manuscripts of the f3 group retain all twenty-four Revelations in both locations: it is a fair assumption that this group, therefore, represents the oldest editorial arrangement of the text.' Secondly, F obeys no consistent system: in Book II the Revelations are retained, in Book VII, they have been left out, while in Book IV the texts are rather haphazardly retained or truncated. It is probably safe to assume that F belongs to a marginally later stratum than 0.72 A third method is followed by Kx73, where all ten Revelations of Book IV and three out of five in Book II are truncated, the text containing a reference to the corresponding passage in Book VIII. Rev. II 12 and 1374, and all seven Revelations of Books VI and VII are geminated. The lack of consistency in referring the reader to Book VIII or retaining the text suo loco shows that this group, too belongs to an early stratum, albeit not so early a one as fl and F. In K, a second scribe, designated K2, has later added the text of the truncated Revelations at the end of the codex, for the express reason that the manuscript should be complete and in all respects identical with those that were per doctores examinatos et defensatos.' So doing, has provided us with the full text of the geminated Revelations, as eventually printed by Gh. Some of his statements are also completely in error, as for instance his observations on Codd. He then concludes that the confusion observed concerning the geminated Revelation is evidence that these two different parts of F go back to different exemplars; 1 find it difficult to follow this reasoning, since all the geminated Revelations belong to the first part postulated.  45 Yet other manuscripts, such as q and Fb, h and S76 (the latter two are not exhibited in Table 1) exhibit an increasing degree of consistency, whereby the where the Revelations are truncated, to reader is referred from Books Book VIII. It is reasonable to take this as a sign that these manuscripts represent later stages in the history of redaction. As mentioned above (section 1.2), Fb, written in 1386, is the oldest dated Revelations manuscript. Since, on the other hand, the very latest possible dating for the complete Liber celestis Reuelacionum (including the ES and Liber VIII) is September 137977 , those manuscripts ex- hibiting what I consider an older arrangement of the geminated Revelations must have been composed (or, at least, copied from a manuscript composed) after this date but considerably before 1386: a very short period of time. The full discussion of this problem will have to be postponed until the full material can be presented, in my forthcoming edition of Liber VIII. Finally, the Editio princeps, Gh, is wholly consistent within itself but adopts the while in opposite system: all Revelations concerned are printed in Books Book VIII the same texts have been truncated and the reader is referred back to the first location. This appears to be the final stage in the development. One Revelation, II 12 (= VIII 36), is consistently doubled in all manuscripts except F. It follows that we have a rather narrow basis for the reconstruction of the oldest Alfonsine text of these Revelations, as only the [3 manuscripts and K2 and Gh retain the full wording, whereas F gives most of the text (Rev. IV 1, 17, 45, 103-105) and q is of use only in Rev. IV 45. Our first task is then to evaluate the text of these manuscripts and the Editio princeps. Alfonso revised the text of these Now, as has been pointed out many Revelations when he added them to Book VIII. The revision mostly took the form of his adding words and phrases that would help make his point more clearly and sometimes considerably changed the wording of the text.' Our first task must therefore be to establish which, if any, of the manuscripts listed best renders the text of these Revelations before they were edited by Alfonso for Book VIII'. A 76 For r, see above, note 16; h = Stockholm, KB, MS. A 70 (Books V— VIII) c. XV late, and MS. A 70a (Books I—IV) a. 1424; S = Stockholm, KB, MS. A 66, a. 1418. 77 JONSSON (1989:108). 78 Cf. WESTMAN (1911:25ff.), KRAFT (1929:134ff.), BERG!! (1967:35 note 4), ÖBERG (1969:9f.), UNDHAGEN (1978:102, note 11), ALLA (1986:83ff.), and JÖNSSON (1989:59ff.) 79 Cf. AHA (1986:83ff.). BERGH (1967:95) compares the readings of I and n (to which he also reckons P) in VII 18, 19 and 30 with the readings of Rev. VIII 22, 23 and 58, and considers the correspondence between n and the Book VIII readings evidence that the n branch offers the better text. In the light of the evidence provided by Book IV, 1 prefer to regard such correspondences as evidence that the n branch represents an early stage of that textual revision which reached its conclusion in Book VIII. Hence, in my view, the readings of (3 are doser to the first Alfonso Redaction. 
 
 
 
 
 46 certain amount of ground has already been covered, chiefly on the nature of Alfonso's editorial activities, and the full discussion of this question must wait until the edition of Book VIII'. A few facts may be summarised here. First, there are numerous instances of additions to the text that can only be observed in those manuscripts — chiefly those of the f3 group — which report the text of Book VIII suo loco. Thus, the text of the rubrics has generally been completely rewritten. In these instances, the wording of the Incipit is often also amplified, as in IV 3:1 "0, Domine," inquit sponsa, which in VIII 41:1 has been changed to Loquebatur sponsa ad Christum orando pro regno Suecie inter alia dicens: "Domine," inquit. Moreover, explanations, clarifying the context and giving it more point, have been added to the text; thus IV 3:16 ad legittimum possessorem is expanded into VIII 41:16 ad legittimum dominum, id est ad illum regem, heredem predecessoris regis concedentis. Secondly, there is another major group of additions which have made their way, not only into the text of Book VIII written suo loco, but also into the corresponding text of Book IV, as reported by various manuscripts and by Gh. The most import- ant additions of this class are': IV 4:3 domine tibi note] domine regine tibi note de qua alias dixi tibi Gh regine de qua iam alias tibi dixi VIII 13:4 IV 4:6 multumque computare] et teneri ad reddendum longam et onerosam racionem K2 Gh et VIII 13:6 IV 4:15 et passim infusio] infusio dyabolica Gh infusio dyaboli VIII 13:15 IV 45:1 maiestate] imperiali maiestate K2 F Gh et VIII 50:1 IV 48:1 iste] iste rex Gh et VIII 7:1 IV 74:15 ergo] ergo miles K2 Gh et VIII 34:15 IV 74:24 deserves extra comment. The Book IV tradition runs: Omnis quippe, qui diligit proximum, tenetur primum dolere, quod omnes, qui sunt redempti sangui- ne Ihesu Christi, non rependunt Deo dileccionem. This wording leaves something to be desired, theologically speaking, as it appears to mean that not everybody has been redeemed by the blood of Christ'. This has led to various corrections: omnes qui has been corrected into omnes (P and VIII 34:24 K) or omnes illi (VIII 34:24 Y); non rependunt Deo dileccionem has been corrected into qui tamen omnes non rependunt Deo dileccionem P (deo om.) and VIII 34:24 K. In this Revelation, P, through an unknown source, has incorporated several corrections otherwise pertai- ning to the tradition of Book VIII. This is also true of BOAb in IV 74:29 caput] domina mea caput BOAb et VIII 34:29, IV 74:32 promerebuntur] promerebunt BOAb K2 et VIII 34:32, IV 74:44 manus] armature manus BOAb et VIII 34:44 Y armature manuum VIII 34:44 K, and IV 74:58 sancti] innumerabiles sancti BOAb and VIII 34:58. xl 82 In preparation by Hans ARA. The text of Book VIII has been collated for this discussion in Mss. Y and K only. Cf. also ARA (1986:87f.). 
 
 
 
 
 47 IV 74:75 possibile esset] possibile esset sine Dei offensa Gh et VIII 34:75 IV 103:1 ad virginem Mariam beatus Dyonisius loquebatur] beatus Dyonisius loquebatur ad Mariam virginem Matrem Domini dicens F et VIII 26:1 IV 103:3 anime] anime infinite F et VIII 26:3 IV 104:15 anime vero eorum] anime vero eorum nudate a corpore F et VIII 27:15 IV 104:16 quia] quia Mater mea carissima F et VIII 27:16 IV 104:16 ista] ista sponsa mea F et VIII 27:16 IV 104:16 astat] astat spiritu F et VIII 27:16 IV 104:17 secunda vox est communitatis, que cotidie rogat me] secunda vox est communitatis et populi, que cotidie rogat me K F et VIII 27:17. This illustrates the chronological order of the additions: in the oldest version, que quite correctly refers to communitatis; in the amplified version, the meaning of this word has been clarified by the addition of et populi, but the gender of the relative pronoun was not modified accordingly into qui. IV 105:1 mittam eis] mittam regibus Francie et Anglie F et VIII 28:1 The oldest version of the text of these ten Revelations is therefore clearly that handed down by the manuscripts of the I group, chiefly V and Y, since, as we have seen, P and BOAb in certain instances have adopted readings also to be found in the Book VIII tradition. K2 and F to an even greater extent contain wordings that have probably been imported from Book VIII. Gh, finally, is a special case, as the text of these Revelations has been truncated in Book VIII: the fragment remaining obviously belongs to the Book VIII tradition, however. On the other hand, the text given by Gh in the corresponding Revelations of Book IV is the same as that handed down by K2. The full investigation into the relations between the tradition of Books II—VII on the one hand and that of Book VIII on the other must await the publication of the text of Book VIII; since the text of these Revelations is to be published twice, in the same manner as it was written in the 13 group of manusc- ripts, the soundest method to be adopted for the present edition will be to publish the oldest discernible version of the text, leaving the more developed Alfonsine version to be printed in the corresponding Revelations in Book VIII. In Book IV, then, the text published is that of the [3 group of manuscripts. 
 
 
 
 
 3 Textual Criticism 3.1 Principles This edition is intended to present the text of the original Alfonsine version, which is generally identical with that of the archetype reconstructed by means of the Stemma codicum, that is, the text correctly handed down in at least three of the four identifiable branches of the textual tradition, PVY, BOAb, Kux, and Fq. When the wording of a rubric has been in doubt, I have also collated the lists of rubrics to Book IV given by PVY BOAb ux q.84 In certain instances, where the correctness of the archetype has been in doubt, I have also turned to sources outside the manuscript tradition of Book IV, such as the medieval retranslation of the Revelations into Old Swedish (Bli)x5 , the text of other Revelations and the Acta et processus canonizacionis Sancte Birgitte (A&P). Reference to these sources is given in the Apparatuses. In those cases where no consensus exists between the manuscripts, my main principle has been to adopt the reading that context or sense would suggest to be the more authentic one. In these instances, I have often had reason to consider the reading retained by PVY BOAb as more authentic, whereas that of Kux Fq gives the impression of representing a later revision. One example of this type is Rev. IV 22:3: Diu enim est quod sedes apostolica non placauit (placuit V Ab) Deum PVY BOAb diu enim est quod sedes apostolica placauit Deum Kux Fq Gh: the construction, diu est quod, where quod functions as a temporal adverb ("it is a long time that"), is attested from archaic Latin, but appears later to have been construed as a temporal conjunction ("it is a long time since")x6. In the former case, the negation (non placauit) is necessary, in the latter, it is superfluous. The choice between the two readings is not obvious, but the reading of PVY BOAb appears to be the more difficult one, inviting a scribe to delete the negation before the verb. The construction, diu est quod, is attested also in Rev. VII 22:1: Filia mea, diu est quod ego promiseram tibi in Roma, quod ostenderem tibi modum partus mei. Here, quod undoubtedly functions as a No list rubrics are given in K or F. For a discussion of the use of these rubrics in the Revelations editions already printed, see BERGH (1967:89ff.) and UNDHAGEN (1978:226) 85 Cf. ÖBERG (1986:68ff.) s6 Cf. LOFSTEDT (1911:56f.) and SZANTYR (1965:580f.). 
 
 
 
 
 49 temporal conjunction. Hence it is even more probable that the n reading of IV 22:3 is an attempt at normalising the text; the contrary assumption — that the 13 scribe has attempted to correct the text by adding non — is therefore less tenable. Cf. also VI 6:4: Cum enim ab aliquo ad iram prouocata fueris, vsquequo ira ab animo semota non fuerit, non loquaris ei ...: non ante fuerit om. 0 F Gh del. q87. This passage illustrates how a misunderstood negation may be deleted to facilitate understanding. The archetype's usquequo ira semota non fuerit "as long as your wrath shall not have been removed from your soul" is no less correct Latin than the text of 0 F and Gh, "until your wrath shall have been removed from your soul". IV 53:6 virginitas viduitas et coniugium VY BOA') virginitatis viduitatis et coniugium P virginitatis viduitatis et coniugum q virginitatis viduitatis et coniugii Kux F Gh. The genitivus explicativus is the more correct reading, grammatically speaking, and would be less liable to correction by scribes; hence, the reading of VY BOA') has the greater air of authority. As mentioned above (ch. 2.1.3), in a certain number of instances the best reading appears to be found in a smaller body of manuscripts, namely VY. Thanks to their close connection with the monastery of San Girolamo di Quarto where Alfonso of Ja&I spent his last few years, the two latter have retained by interpolation or correction a greater number of authentic readings than the other manuscripts of their branch of the tradition. Instances where I have considered the reading of VY superior to that or those of the rest of the tradition are: IV 4:26 turri VY turrim BOAb K2 Gh carcerem P: turri is also the reading of Y and K in VIII 13:26 IV 11:11 fecisti monti tuo cohabitare VY2 fecisti monti tuo P fecisti monti tuo habitare B monti tuo fecisti habitare 0 fecisti in monte tuo habitare post torr. Ab cohabitare fecisti monti tuo Kux Fq cohabitari fecisti monti tuo Gh. Except for VY2, the 3 tradition has lost the prefix co- (P has also lost habitare); in n on the other hand the word order appears to have been adjusted. IV 13:7 dispendia animarum VY dispendia anime P BOAb Kux Fq Gh: cf. IV 12:2 de dispendio animarum and 17:2 dispendium animarum. IV 17:16 viuificabit VY iudicabit P BOAb K2 F Gh: cf 1 Sm. 2,6 and 4 Rg. 5,7. IV 17:25 cuius angelus tuus est auriga VY cuius angelus tuus est P cuius angelus tuus auriga est BOAb cuius auriga angelus tuus est K2 F Gh. The manuscripts of the 13 group have a long hyperbaton between cuius and auriga (the latter word is omitted by P), the most extreme being found in VY; this apparently gave rise to corrections in the ir manuscripts. IV 53:1 sue VY eius P BOAb Kux Fq Gh: sue is, by a narrow margin, the more difficult reading, and would therefore be more liable to correction. 87 Cf. BERGII (1991:65). 
 
 
 
 
 50 IV 53:4 desiderauit VY desiderabat P BOAb Kux Fq Gh. Since all the verbs of this clause are in the perfect tense, desiderabat appears the more likely to be an error. 3.2 Problematical passages IV 3:3 erant in diluuio in archa (in archa del.) Y erant in diluuio PV BOAb q erant in archa noe K2 Gh. Rev. VIII 41:3: post erant in ras. in archa noe scripta vid. Y, in archa noe r. In the Book IV tradition, erant in diluuio appears to have been the reading of the archetype, the reading of K2 Gh being a later, rather obvious correction. The reading of Y, erant in diluuio in archa, is more problematical, as in archa has not been added but has instead been deleted on a later occasion. I have found no instances where the first hand of Y corrects against his source — the corrections are all done by Y2; hence the reading was in all likelihood present in the exemplar of Y. In Book VIII 41:3, the words in archa noe in Y appear to have been written by a different hand, possibly in rasura, whereas r has in archa noe. Since the words in diluuio alone are plainly in error, and the reading of K2 Gh and Book VIII, in archa noe, are a suspected later correction, it is reasonable to assume that the reading retained by Y at the very least represents a more economical and possibly more authoritative correction of the archetype error. IV 7:28 debent Kux Gh dicunt PVY BOAb ex dicunt torr. F in ras. scr. q: dicunt is an error of the archetype, debent being a plausible correction. IV 21:1 trinus in personis et unus in natura: et unus BOAb x unus u unum PVY K Fq Gh. Cf. Rev. VIII 1:7 Itaque Deus trinus est et unus: trinus in personis, unus in natura. IV 23:4 est factus sanctus V BOAb factus est sanctus q est factus PY (sanctus ante factus in marg. suppl. Y 2) factus est suus ux est factus simulatorie sanctus K factus est F (post quae verba simulatorie sanctus in marg. add. F2) factus est simulatorie sanctus Gh. This passage deals with a person whom the Devil deludes into attemp- ting impossible feats; among other things, he is falsely informed that he has been made completely holy: this statement has been modified in K, F, and Gh by the rather pointless addition of simulatorie. In view of the presence of sanctus in all manuscripts except ux and the conflicting word order of K F, there can be no doubt that factus est sanctus is the reading of the archetype. It is remarkable, however, that the reading of ux, factus est suus (a misunderstood abbreviation, or possibly a "Verschlimmbesserung") is identical with that of BU: "Vtan thässom bär ondir ande vitne at han är allir hans" — "But to him the evil spirit bears witness that he is altogether his". IV 24:1 dolium vini BOAb Kux Fq Gh dolium vini vel ceruisie PVY. I have followed the wording of the archetype, as the words vel ceruisie could easily have been supplemented by PVY from §2. The Latin text of the three first sections of this Revelation is much wordier than the Old Swedish text, and it is quite possible that the latter, which at this point only mentions "ölkar" ("a vat of ale -) represents 
 
 
 
 
 51 a version doser to Birgitta's original text. In the latter part of the Revelation (sections 4-8) it is, on the other hand, the Latin version that gives the greater impression of authority.88 IV 24:2 minuendum: cf. Am (1986, note 16). IV 32:8 trinus et F deus et q et deus Ua om. K Gh. Cf. IV 21:1 above. IV 33:18 et tolate] et colate u in colate x. I have interpreted tolate, clearly handed down from the archetype, as a false reading of collatet9, the perfect participle of confero, and more or less synonymous with the preceding arte'. This interpretation also agrees with that of BU ad loc.: "Ok thera ärma äru nu thranga oc kröpada" ("And their sleeves are now tight and shrunk"). IV 60:2 tribulatus dicit ima pag. suppl. K2 om. rodd. Gh. A necessary addition, given by K2. IV 102:22 In this dialogue between the Judge and the Devil we should expect the Devil's arguments in the beginning of §22 to be answered by the Judge at the end of the same section. Since no reply is to be found, we must suspect that a passage of the original text has been lost in transmission. The wording of Kux qUa is Et Ethiops: Tu, inquit, dixisti). F (Gh) offers a correction by inserting addidit after Ethiops ("And the negro added"). In BU (II, 192, 20-25), the problematical passage has instead been condensed into "swa som thu sagdhe" — "just as you said". Since the text offered by the majority of the manuscripts is obviously defective, and F (Gh) and BU offer individual corrections to this problem, we may conclude that the text was corrupt already in the archetype. IV 126:34 propterea quod V propterea qui PY BOA') Kux Fq Gh: the subject of the entire clause (in §33), illud quod trahitur, is in the neuter, as is evident also in §34, donet trahibile et delectabile fiat. Hence, whether by direct influence from Alfonso's original or by correction, V alone has the correct reading. IV 136:R Clementem VI x q Clementem V Ku F om. Gh. Clement V (1305 —1314) could not possibly have been the recipient of Revelations by St. Birgitta, whereas Clement VI (1342-1352) held office at the time of Birgitta's peregrination to Santiago de Compostela (1341-1343), when she passed through France.  52 3.3 Emendations In emending the text I have followed a very restrictive policy, preferring in many, if not most, cases to comment upon difficult passages in the Apparatus criticus, sometimes suggesting a better reading by the word exspectes. In the main, my emendations concern minor errors, readily explained as paleo- graphical mistakes or slips of the pen. In the following instances, I have considered the textual tradition corrupt and have added or deleted a word or changed the word order of the archetype. Among the emendations I have also counted those in- stances where I have adopted the reading given by a source outside the manuscript tradition, such as BU. IV 2:3 cuius os scripsi os codd. Gh IV 15:28 post moriturus addidi et om. codd. Gh IV 16:26 eiusmodi exclusi auctore BU ad loc., habent PVY BOA' ux q ex eiusdemmodi corr. F eiusdem K huiusmodi Gh IV 17:19 subiturus scripsi facturus codd. Gh et VIII 14:19. I have corrected the text, which appears to be an archetype error, on the strength of the wording of IV 17:22: ne grauius iudicium subeam. BU (III, 329, 28) corrects the text, by assuming racionem in place of iudicium, into: "fore gudz dom görande rekinskap aff allo mik andwordhadho" — "Before God's judgement giving a reckoning of all that had been entrusted to me." IV 136:0 sexto scripsi quinto Ku Fq om. x deest Gh Rev. VI:63 ed. BERGH. Cf. note above (sect. 3.2) on 136:R. IV 142:14 fortitudine scripsi fortitudinem codd. Gh: the passive voice of induo is usually constructed with the ablative in the Revelations'; cf. IV 6:9 induentur confusione, and RS 124 cum autem induitur cuculla. Another construction of the sentence under discussion, consurge viriliter induere fortitudinem confidenter, whe- re induere is regarded as an infinitivus finalis, appears less likely, since this con- struction cannot be found at all in the Revelations. 92 I am much indebted to Prof. BERGH, who pointed out this usage to me. 
 
 
 
 
 4 Principles of Edition The orthography of the text published in this volume follows in most details that of Codex Kalmarnensis; the orthography of the manuscripts not being homogeneous, it appeared to me problematical to normalise the spelling throughout. The Codex Kalmarnensis, on the other hand, is one of the oldest surviving Vadstena manusc- ripts of the Revelaciones and therefore represents the orthography practised in the mother foundation of the Birgittine order at the end of the 14th century. The Codex Falkenberg is probably older than the Codex Kalmarnensis; however, it lacks a sizeable portion of the text through the loss of an entire quire, and it would have been necessary to reconstruct its orthography in this part. I therefore consider that by my accepting the main traits of the orthography of K, my edition may indeed perhaps be considered to have preserved a certain flavour of Vadstena Abbey that would be absent in a strictly normalised text. In two cases, however, 1 have diverged from the usage of K: by printing vocalic u (not v, which dominates in K) and by printing membrum, not menbrum, although the latter spelling is marginally more common in the rather small number of cases where K has written this word out in full. These rules I have applied even to those portions of the text absent in K or written by K2 at the end of the codex. The orthographical rules of the first hand of K and those of K2 are not fully identical, however; the main divergences being that where K writes abundo, loqutus, and obtineo, K2 writes habundo, locutus, and optineo. The manuscripts used for this edition exhibit a very great number of errors and variant readings, not all of which are relevant for an understanding of the history of the text. In order to restrict the size of the apparatus criticus for the main body of the text, namely Rev. IV 1-130 (excluding the Add., Decl. , and the geminated Revelations) I therefore only report a selection of the readings, using the following principles. 1. In the main text of Rev. IV 1-130, I report the most important individual readings of those manuscripts which I judge crucial to an understanding of the tradition, namely P, V and Y. 2. The readings of the two manuscripts originating in the scriptorium of Vadstena Abbey, namely F and K, may have a significant part to play in a future project of 
 
 
 
 
 54 producing a new edition of the Old Swedish version of the Reuelaciones, Birgittas uppenbarelser (BU); I therefore also report the most important of their readings. 3. The Editio princeps, Gh, has been used as the basic edition of the Reuelaciones for so long that its readings, whether erroneous or correct, are widely quoted as if they constituted the canonical text of the Reuelaciones. I therefore consider it important to give modern readers the opportunity of comparing the readings of Gh with those of the manuscript tradition, and consequently I also report most rea- dings of this edition. 4. The readings of the remaining manuscripts collated for this edition are repor- ted only when they are present in an entire group: in the apparatus criticus, the individual readings of B, 0, Ab, q, u, and x are thus generally only reported when shared by other manuscripts. I have made an exception to this rule in the case of very long omissions of text, since these may be helpful in establishing the relation- ships and textual worth of any newly discovered manuscripts'. In those portions of the text retained only by a few manuscripts, namely the Add., Dec!., geminated Revelations, and Rev. IV 131-144, I generally report the variant readings and errors of all manuscripts collated. As in the editions published by HOLLMAN, BERGH, EKLUND, and UNDHAGEN, the text has been subdivided into sections. The subdivisions used in the Ghotan edition — capital letters inserted into the text — are given in the margin. While these editions generally refer to passages of the text by page numbers, chapters and lines, I have made chapters and sections the basis of all references in the Apparatus fontium and Apparatus criticus as weil as in the Introduction and Indexes94. This has perforce led to a certain wordiness in the apparatus, as my ambition has been at all costs to avoid ambiguity. In the apparatus criticus, the lemma, if given to clarify the reference, is the subject of all comments; when no lemma is given, the subject is generally obvious. Thus, in IV 127:16 abieccioneml deieccionem K2 post torr. q, the latter manuscript, after correcting the text, reads abieccionem. Besides u, which was virtually unknown to Birgitta scholars, until it was found by Prof. EKLUND and (later) by myself, a hitherto unknown Revelations manuscript was discovered in 1985. A description, in the form of a letter from Dr. M. MAIROLD, Graz, to Dr. U. MONTAG, Miinchen, was circulated in 1985 through the good services of Dr. T. NYBERG, Odense. This manuscript, which has as yet not been assigned a siglum, is: (Dependance der Grazer Zentralbibliothek in) Giissing, Bibliothek der Fran- ziskaner, a. 1445. By collating a microfilm copy of this manuscript, I have found its text to be closely related to that of Ab. 94 The same policy has been adopted by BERGH in his edition of Book VI.

 60 B LIBRI QVARTI CAP. 1 aurum cetera precellit metalla, sic scriptura, quam vos dicitis sanc- tam, nos vero vocamus eam auream, precellit omnes scripturas, 5 quia in ipsa honoratur et predicatur verus Deus, patriarcharum repli- cantur opera, prophetarum explanantur infusiones. 6 Ergo quia nullum opus sine remuneracione est, audi que loquor: Rex iste coram Deo predator est et proditor animarum prodigusque effusor diuiciarum. 7 Itaque sicut nullus proditor est peior illo qui diligentem se prodit, sic iste multos spiritualiter prodidit, carnaliter diligendo iniustos, iniuste exaltando impios, lustos deprimendo, excessus corrigendos dissimulando. 8 Secundo nullus predo peior est illo qui prodit eum, qui in sinu eius caput suum reclinat. Sic communitas terre quasi in sinu suo erat, quam rex iste miserabiliter depredatus est 9 aliorum bona auferri permittendo, aliis intolerabilia imponendo, aliorum iniurias dissimulando, iusticiam semper remisse exercendo. Tercio nullus fur peior est illo qui creditis sibi omnibus et acceptis clauibus furatur domino inuito. 10 Sic iste accepit claues potestatis et honoris, quibus iniuste et prodigaliter usus est, non ad honorem Dei. Ergo quia aliqua sibi placita dimisit amore mei, consulo sibi tria. 11 Primo quod sit sicut ille euangelicus qui relictis siliquis porcorum rediit ad patrem. Sic ipse contempnat diuicias et honores, 12 que respectu eternorum non sunt nisi porcorum silique, cum humilitate et deuocione regrediendo ad patrem suum Deum. Secundo dimittat mortuos sepelire mortuos suos et sequatur viam artam crucifixi Dei. 13 Tercio relinquat graue pondus peccatorum suorum et ingrediatur per viam illam, que in principio est arta set in fine gaudiosa. 11 siliquis porcorum: Lc. 15,16 8,22 12 dimittat mortuos sepelire mortuos suos: Mt. 5 deus verus 4 vocamus eam] vocamus eam in celo K2 Gh in celo vocamus eam F predator] 6 post iste add. pro quo tu supplicas K2 Gh in marg. add.  LIBRI QVARTI CAP. 1-2 61 14 Tu quoque, que vides me, intellige, quia ego sum ille, qui scripturam auream plenius agnoui et agnoscendo augmentaui. Ego fui nudatus ignominiose, set quia pacienter sustinui, Deus vestiuit animam meam veste immortali. 15 Ego eciam fui intinctus oleo, ideo nunc gaudeo oleo leticie sempiterne. Ego insuper post Matrem Dei leuissima morte de mundo transiui, quia custos Matris Dei factus fui, et corpus meum est in loco quietissimo et securissimo." Visio mirabilis et notanda sponse et qualiter Deus ipsam visfonem expo- nit; in qua exposicione baptizati per animal, gentiles per piscem et Dei amici per tres turbas designantur. Capitulum 1 Post hec videbantur sponse quasi due statere stantes iuxta terram, quarum summitates et ligature ascendebant nubes, circuli vero ipsi penetrabant celum. 2 In prima vero statera erat piscis, cuius squame acute erant quasi rasorium, visus eius quasi basilisci, os vero quasi unicornii effundentis venenum, aures autem quasi acutissime lancee et quasi lamine ferree. 3 In alia vero statera erat animal quoddam, cuius pellis quasi silex, <cuius> os ingens erat effundens flammas ardentes, cuius palpebre quasi durissimi gladii, cuius aures durissime erant emittentes sagittas acutissimas tanquam de arcu duro et extenso. 4 Post hec apparuerunt tres turbe populorum in terra; prima erat modica, secunda minor, tercia vero minima.  62 B LIBRI QVARTI CAP. 2 mirabilis, si quis esset, qui ex caritate presentaret michi illud. Ego eciam feruentissime concupisco sanguinem piscis, dummodo inue- niretur homo, qui eum afferret michi." 6 Respondit vox una de turba loquens quasi ex ore omnium: "Audi," inquit, "o conditor noster. Quomodo poterimus tibi presentare cor animalis tanti, cuius pellis durior est silice? 7 Si appropinquare voluerimus ori eius, incendemur flamma ignis eius; si viderimus oculos eius, transfodiemur scintillis sagittarum eius; 8 et si forte spes aliqua esset de animali habendo, quis poterit apprehendere piscem, cuius squame acuciores sunt cuspidibus, cuius oculi extinguunt visum nostrum, cuius os diffundit in nos venenum insanabile?" 9 Respondit vox de celo dicens: "0, amici, animal et piscis videretur vobis inuincibiles, set apud omnipotentem est facile. Ergo quicumque querit viam ad impugnandum animal, ego de celo infundam sapienciam fortitudinemque tribuam. Qui vero paratus est pro me mori, ego ipse me dabo ei in remuneracionem." 10 Respondit turba prima: "0, summe pater, tu es dator tocius boni. Nos vero, factura tua, libenter dabimus tibi cor nostrum ad honorem tuum; cetera vero, que extra cor sunt, disponemus ad sustentacionem et refeccionem carnis nostre. 11 Et quia mors videtur nobis dura, infirmitas carnis onerosa, sciencia vero modica, ideo rege nos interius et exterius, accipeque gratanter que offerimus et repende quantum et quidquid tibi placet." 12 Respondit turba secunda: "Recognoscimus infirmitates nostras et attendimus mundi vanitates et varietates. Ideo libenter dabimus tibi cor nostrum omnemque voluntatem nostram relinquimus in manus aliorum, quia subesse aliis magis desideramus quam minima de mundo possidere." 6 omnium] michi illud ex caritate presentaret ux quis] aliquis Kux Fq Gh si] si vero incedemur V 7 ori eius] eius ori u huius ori x ostendens V 9 ux 8 post squame add. et ale seu pennicule piscis Gh supra lin. add.
